License: CC BY 4.0
arXiv:2605.28099v1 [stat.ME] 27 May 2026
A computationally-tractable measure of global sensitivity for sampling-based Bayesian inference
Arina Odnoblyudova
Corresponding author: arina.odnoblyudova.24@ucl.ac.uk
Department of Statistical Science, University College London
Charita Dellaporta
Department of Statistical Science, University College London
FranГ§ois-Xavier Briol
Department of Statistical Science, University College London
Abstract
Bayesian inference can often be sensitive to the choice of hyperparameters of the prior or likelihood, yet defining and quantifying this sensitivity in a principled and computationally feasible way remains challenging in practice. Unfortunately, existing sensitivity methods are rarely applicable in modern Bayesian workflows due to their high computational cost and poor performance in moderate to high dimensions. To address these limitations, we introduce a new approach to global sensitivity analysis based on the Fisher divergence. Our method only requires a set of samples from a reference posterior and the ability to evaluate score functions, making it broadly computationally tractable. Under mild regularity conditions, it controls changes in the whole posterior, and provides a bound on the impact of perturbations on the first two moments. We demonstrate these strengths on challenging Bayesian inference problems which are practically out of reach of existing approaches, including generalised Bayesian inference for unnormalised models, inference in Bayesian models of time series, and neural simulation-based inference.
1
INTRODUCTION
Bayesian posterior distributions can be highly sensitive to modelling choices, particularly prior and likelihood hyperparameters. This sensitivity may substantially affect uncertainty quantification and predictive performance
(Berger
et al.
,
1994
; Ruggeri
et al.
,
2005
; Kleijn and Van Der Vaart,
2006
; Owhadi
et al.
,
2015
)
, and may arise even when modelling choices vary within a plausible range. This challenge is further amplified in generalisations
(Bissiri
et al.
,
2016
; Knoblauch
et al.
,
2022
)
or approximations
(Cranmer
et al.
,
2020
)
of Bayesian inference, which frequently introduce additional hyperparameters whose influence is hard to assess. Consequently, evaluating sensitivity is a key component of the Bayesian workflow
(Gelman
et al.
,
2020
; Depaoli
et al.
,
2020
)
.
This task falls within the scope of Bayesian sensitivity analysis
(Berger,
1990
; Ruggeri
et al.
,
2005
)
, with existing methods generally categorised into local and global approaches, both of which analyse sensitivity relative to a reference prior/likelihood pair. The local approach
(Gustafson
et al.
,
1996
; Gustafson,
2000
; Roos
et al.
,
2015
; Al-Labadi
et al.
,
2021
; Giordano
et al.
,
2023
; Kallioinen
et al.
,
2024
; Di Noia
et al.
,
2025
)
employs differential techniques to assess the effect of infinitesimal perturbations around this reference. It is well studied and often computationally tractable, but its interpretation can be less intuitive and its relevance limited to small perturbations. In contrast, global sensitivity analysis
(Berger and Berliner,
1986
; Moreno,
2000
; Kurtek and Bharath,
2015
; Ghaderinezhad
et al.
,
2022
; Ho,
2023
)
, the main focus of this paper, aligns more closely with practical concerns: it evaluates the maximum change in the quantity of interest within a neighbourhood of the reference choice. Practitioners often have a sense of a plausible range of alternatives, and this approach directly quantifies how much their inferences could change within that realistic range.
Existing global sensitivity approaches can be categorised according to three main design choices: a measure used to quantify sensitivity, such as a posterior functional or a statistical divergence to a reference posterior, a neighbourhood in which sensitivity is measured, and a computational tool through which the method is implemented, typically combining an estimator for the sensitivity measure and a numerical optimiser. These algorithmic components interact, leading to significant trade-offs between three key desiderata: (i)
the strength
of the sensitivity measure, i.e. whether the measure controls changes in the entire posterior or only some functionals such as the mean, (ii) the
interpretability
of the measure, and (iii) the
computational tractability
of the method.
To date, no existing method has successfully balanced these desiderata in a way that is practically relevant for most modern Bayesian modelling problems, where the dimensionality of the posterior can be large, and posterior computation is expensive. The biggest limitation has been computational, with many existing methods either being limited to low dimensions
(Moreno and Cano,
1991
; Moreno,
2000
)
, requiring a conjugate posterior
(Berger
et al.
,
2000
)
, requiring a large number of runs of a sampling method
(Kurtek and Bharath,
2015
; Ghaderinezhad
et al.
,
2022
)
, or requiring the solution of challenging optimisation problems
(Wasserman
et al.
,
1993
; Lavine
et al.
,
2000
)
.
Consequently, global sensitivity analysis is rarely applied in practice. Instead, many practitioners adopt an informal approach: they evaluate a few variants around a reference model and informally assess their impact on the posterior. While straightforward, this approach can easily overlook other plausible modelling choices that may lead to substantially different inferences.
Our goal in this paper is to address the computational challenges that have limited the practical use of global sensitivity analysis. To this end, we propose a new sensitivity measure based on the
Fisher divergence
(FD)
(HyvГӨrinen,
2005
)
, a statistical divergence which controls changes in the entire posterior. This measure is both strong and interpretable: under regularity conditions, it provides a bound on the difference in the first two moments and on changes in total variation, Kullback-Leibler and Wasserstein distance. The key advantage of the FD, however, is its computational tractability. It can be estimated using only a single set of samples from the reference posterior, without requiring repeated posterior sampling for each perturbed model in the sensitivity neighbourhood. This is achieved through evaluations of the score of these perturbed posteriors, a key quantity used for many methods in Bayesian computation, including algorithms based on Langevin dynamics and Hamiltonian Monte Carlo
(Hoffman and Gelman,
2014
; Barp
et al.
,
2018
; Fearnhead
et al.
,
2024
)
. Furthermore, the FD has computational complexity which is linear in the number of posterior samples and in the dimensionality of the posterior, and can be approximated at a rate depending on the square root of the number of samples, which is dimension-free. This makes it much better suited for measuring sensitivity in high-dimensional posteriors. Armed with this sensitivity measure, we consider two settings. The first, most general, consists of performing global optimisation over the space of hyperparameters, allowing us to consider a broad range of problems of practical relevance. The second, more limited, case considers neighbourhoods constructed through hypercubes of hyperparameters in exponential family priors. The latter setting does however allow us to recast the optimisation problem in a tractable form which can be solved exactly in a finite number of evaluations of the FD.
We demonstrate the widespread applicability of this approach through extensive experiments on problems which are out of range of all existing methods. This includes Bayesian inference for heavy-tailed time-series, amortised neural simulation based inference for simulators of time-series in telecommunication engineering, and generalisations of Bayesian inference, which we consider in the context of challenging doubly-intractable models including the Ising model.
2

Although a large number of methods have been proposed over the last four decades, these tend to suffer from the same three computational challenges:
вҖў
Firstly, for a given candidate posterior

~
О»

Similarly,

For this reason, most Bayesian global sensitivity methods rely on simpler posterior functionals such as posterior moments

\mathcal{O}(m^{-1/2})
, but unfortunately do not necessarily capture changes in the entire posterior.
вҖў
Secondly, beyond the difficulty of estimating
ПҒ
\rho
once, many existing methods require estimating it repeatedly for different candidates
О 
~
О»
1
,
О 
~
О»
2
,
вҖҰ
\tilde{\Pi}^{\lambda_{1}},\tilde{\Pi}^{\lambda_{2}},\ldots
for
О»
1
,
О»
2
,
вҖҰ
вҲҲ
Оӣ
\lambda_{1},\lambda_{2},\ldots\in\Lambda
throughout the optimisation procedure. This often leads to the use of computationally expensive sampling schemes, with most existing methods requiring sampling from every candidate considered
(Zhu
et al.
,
2011
; Kurtek and Bharath,
2015
; Giacomini
et al.
,
2025
; Ghaderinezhad
et al.
,
2022
)
. One alternative is to avoid this through importance sampling using samples from
О 
~
ref
\tilde{\Pi}_{\text{ref}}
, or to use a few iterations of sequential Monte Carlo; see e.g.
Ho (
2023
)
. However, such approaches can lead to high-variance estimators of
ПҒ
вҖӢ
(
О 
~
О»
)
\rho(\tilde{\Pi}^{\lambda})
, especially for high-dimensional posteriors and when the reference has lighter tails than the candidates.
вҖў
Thirdly, solving the optimisation problems in (
4
) can be very challenging, particularly when the optimisation objective is non-convex.
Several approaches have been proposed to tackle this issue; see for example the linearisation algorithms of
(Lavine,
1991
; Lavine
et al.
,
2000
)
which turn sensitivity measures constructed through posterior expectations into the solution to a sequence of linear optimisation problems. However, most papers bypass the issue entirely by taking a neighbourhood with a finite number of elements, allowing for a direct solution to the supremum and infimum in
Equation
4
(Kurtek and Bharath,
2015
; Ghaderinezhad
et al.
,
2022
; Ho,
2023
)
. This severely limits the applicability of these methods, and makes them much closer to the informal approach to sensitivity analysis.
Put together, these computational challenges mean that existing global sensitivity methods are not well-suited for modern Bayesian inference problems.
2.2
The Fisher Divergence
This paper will tackle some of the aforementioned computational limitations by introducing a sensitivity measure based on the
Fisher divergence (FD)
(HyvГӨrinen,
2005
)
, also called the HyvГӨrinen or score-matching divergence. The FD measures how different two distributions are by comparing their score functions. Given two probability measures
О 
1
,
О 
2
вҲҲ
рқ’«
вҖӢ
(
Оҳ
)
\Pi_{1},\Pi_{2}\in\mathcal{P}(\Theta)
with corresponding scores
s
ПҖ
1
,
s
ПҖ
2
s_{\pi_{1}},s_{\pi_{2}}
, the FD is given by
FD
вҖӢ
(
О 
1
вҲҘ
О 
2
)
\displaystyle\mathrm{FD}(\Pi_{1}\|\Pi_{2})
:=
рқ”ј
Оё
вҲј
О 
1
вҖӢ
[
вҖ–
s
ПҖ
1
вҖӢ
(
Оё
)
вҲ’
s
ПҖ
2
вҖӢ
(
Оё
)
вҖ–
2
2
]
,
\displaystyle:=\mathbb{E}_{\theta\sim\Pi_{1}}\left[\|s_{\pi_{1}}(\theta)-s_{\pi_{2}}(\theta)\|_{2}^{2}\right],
where
вҲҘ
вӢ…
вҲҘ
2
\|\cdot\|_{2}
denotes the Euclidean norm.
For the FD to be well-defined and a statistical divergence, that is,
FD
вҖӢ
(
О 
1
вҲҘ
О

)
=
0
вҮ”
О

О

\mathrm{FD}(\Pi_{1}\|\Pi_{2})=0\iff\Pi_{1}\equiv\Pi_{2}
for all
О 
1
,
О

вҲҲ
рқ’«
вҖӢ
(
Оҳ
)
\Pi_{1},\Pi_{2}\in\mathcal{P}(\Theta)
, certain regularity conditions are required, which we discuss in detail in Section
3.1
.
As we will see in
Section
4
, the FD can be used to upper bound (under certain regularity conditions) the (1 or 2)-Wasserstein or total variation distances between
О 
1
\Pi_{1}
and
О 
2
\Pi_{2}
,
making it a particularly strong notion of discrepancy
(Huggins
et al.
,
2018
)
. The FD is also computationally convenient as it can be expressed as an expectation and can therefore be estimated through a Monte Carlo estimator based on samples from
О 
1
\Pi_{1}
.
The Fisher divergence has been widely used across statistics and machine learning, including for parameter estimation
(HyvГӨrinen,
2005
,
2007
; Barp
et al.
,
2019
; Yu
et al.
,
2019
; Scealy and Wood,
2022
)
, generalised Bayesian inference
(Altamirano
et al.
,
2023
,
2024
)
, in the context of scoring rules
(Parry
et al.
,
2012
)
, and for score-based generative modelling
(Song
et al.
,
2021
)
. In these settings, practitioners typically apply integration by parts when constructing Monte Carlo estimators of the FD, as this removes the dependence on the score of
О 
1
\Pi_{1}
, an object that is usually unavailable. In our setting, the unnormalised posterior density is known up to a constant, so its score can be evaluated directly, and no integrationвҖ‘byвҖ‘parts trick is required. Moreover, whereas most existing applications compute the FD between distributions on the data space
рқ’і
\mathcal{X}
, we apply it to distributions on the parameter space
Оҳ
\Theta
. This makes our use of the FD most closely related to its role in Bayesian model comparison and selection
(Dawid and Musio,
2015
; Shao
et al.
,
2019
; Jewson and Rossell,
2022
)
.
3
METHODOLOGY
We are now ready to propose our novel measure of global sensitivity based on the FD, explore optimisation routines, and discuss interpretability of the measure.

The key advantage of this estimator is that it only relies on the score functions

The main advantage of a convex objective is that, depending on the geometry of the neighbourhood

Similarly, the infimum problem is a convex minimisation problem over a convex and compact set. One approach to solving this is to compute the unconstrained global minimiser

A special case of a convex polytope relevant for Bayesian sensitivity analysis is when

\Gamma

О»
вҖІ

О“

\displaystyle\Gamma_{\text{box}}:=\left\{\lambda\in\Lambda:\lambda^{\prime}_{j}\leq\lambda_{j}\leq\lambda^{\prime\prime}_{j}\text{ for }j\in\{1,\ldots,d_{\Lambda}\}\right\}.
(13)
This is a bounded convex polytope where the constraints are placed separately on each hyperparameter. Since a box-constrained neighbourhood has at most
K
=
2
d
Оӣ
K=2^{d_{\Lambda}}
vertices, computing the supremum for box-constrained sensitivity analysis is computationally inexpensive for small to moderate
d
Оӣ
d_{\Lambda}
, but may be infeasible for large
d
Оӣ

. If the infimum is computed using projected gradient descent, the cost of the projection step onto
О“

\Gamma_{\text{box}}
is
рқ’Ә
вҖӢ
(

)

, since it reduces to coordinate-wise clipping.
Before concluding, we emphasise that the choice of parametrisation is important here. For example, although natural exponential family priors lead to a convex quadratic form, this may not be the case for other parametrisations of an exponential family model. For exponential families in minimal form, this is not a problem as there always exists a smooth and invertible mapping between parametrisations (see e.g. Section 8.1 in
Barndorff-Nielsen (
2014
)
). However, specifying a neighbourhood in natural parameter space may be challenging, and it is not always possible to guarantee that a neighbourhood specified in the original parametrisation will remain a bounded convex polytope once mapped to the natural parametrisation. One exception is affine transformations, which maintain the required geometry of the domain.
4
STRENGTH AND INTERPRETABILITY
We have now proposed a measure of sensitivity which is highly tractable in the sense that it can be estimated without suffering from a curse of dimensionality, and where the optimisation problem can be solved efficiently in a broad range of settings relevant for Bayesian and generalised Bayesian inference. The remaining questions are whether the measure of sensitivity is meaningful and whether it is interpretable to the user.
4.1
Strength of the Measure of Sensitivity
We have already seen in
Proposition
1
that our measure is based on a statistical divergence, and therefore controls changes in the entire posterior. This makes it a much stronger measure than one based only on moments, or any other quantity which measures differences based on summaries. Below, we now show that it is in fact possible to relate our sensitivity measure to sensitivity measures based on alternative statistical divergences which are widely used. However, this is only possible with stronger assumptions on the neighbourhood.
Assumption 4
(Candidate posterior regularity)
.
We assume that
(A4.1)
For all
О»
вҲҲ
О“
\lambda\in\Gamma
,

,

The first assumption corresponds to requiring that the candidate posteriors are strongly log-concave

)
outside of some region near the origin. We note that the product of two functions is log-concave if and only if the functions are both log-concave, so sufficient conditions for this are for the loss function and prior to both be log-concave outside of some region near the origin. In particular, natural exponential family models are log-concave everywhere if and only if the base measure is log-concave and the sufficient statistic is affine. This case includes numerous examples, such as the Gaussian, exponential and Laplace distributions. There are also many examples where the distributions are not exponential family models but are still log-concave in the tails, such as for the Student-t and Gamma distributions. The second assumption simply requires the candidate posteriors to be bounded above and below on the centre of the distribution, which is very mild and will hold when the candidate priors and likelihoods are also bounded above and below on that interval. Under these assumptions, the FD sensitivity controls sensitivity in three widely studied divergences.

4.2
Interpretability of the Measure of Sensitivity
Before concluding, we also consider the interpretability of our measure of sensitivity.
As for all divergence-based Bayesian global sensitivity measures, it is hard to interpret the raw value of
S
FD
вҖӢ
(
О“
)
S^{\text{FD}}(\Gamma)
. Several approaches have been taken to mitigate this issue in the literature. For instance, when measuring sensitivity to the prior, one approach is to normalise the values by e.g. dividing by the value of the divergence evaluated at the worst-case prior in the neighbourhood
(Mingo
et al.
,
2026
)
. This would be inconvenient in our case as the approach is limited to prior sensitivity and would require additional sampling from
О 
ref
\Pi_{\text{ref}}
.
Due to these well-known limitations, we instead propose to focus on relative comparisons. Given two neighbourhoods
О“
1
,
О“
2
вҠӮ
Оӣ
\Gamma_{1},\Gamma_{2}\subset\Lambda
, we can use
S
FD
вҖӢ
(
О“
1
)
вҲ’
S
FD
вҖӢ
(
О“
2
)
S^{\text{FD}}(\Gamma_{1})-S^{\text{FD}}(\Gamma_{2})
to measure the relative sensitivity to
О“
1
\Gamma_{1}
and
О“
2
\Gamma_{2}
. In addition, for a single neighbourhood, the sensitivity measure typically simplifies through various decompositions which can shed more light on what is being measured, including the relative sensitivity to the prior and loss function, or the relative sensitivity to different hyperparameters.

This type of decomposition across parameters is not only interesting for interpretability, but also for computation. Indeed, this allows us to compute our measure of sensitivity separately per dimension, which could significantly lower the dimensionality of each of the optimisation problems, and therefore accelerate convergence.
5
EXPERIMENTS
We are now ready to evaluate the proposed FD-based sensitivity measure. The code to reproduce the experiments is provided
https://github.com/jularina/fd-sense
and additional details are in
Appendix
C
. All experiments were run on macOS (Apple Silicon, M1 Pro) using a single CPU core without GPU acceleration. We used the packages
sbi
(Tejero-Cantero
et al.
,
2020
)
,
POT
(Python Optimal Transport)
(Flamary
et al.
,
2021
)
, and
scipy.optimize
(Virtanen
et al.
,
2020
)
for posterior inference, optimal transport computations, and numerical optimisation, respectively.
We also use
PosteriorDB
(Magnusson
et al.
,
2025
)
to access benchmark Bayesian models.
5.1
Synthetic Experiments with Conjugate Gaussian Location Models
We first study the sensitivity of synthetic conjugate Gaussian location models to prior hyperparameters. The advantage of this toy setting is that conjugacy allows us to study in closed-form the impact of hyperparameters, and thus verify that our method behaves as expected. It also gives us an opportunity to benchmark our approach with existing global sensitivity methods. For this example, we have verified that the Assumptions

,

.

In summary, the FD is able to capture sensitivity through changes in the posterior geometry, which first moment-only summaries can miss. Although this could be easily remedied in this conjugate Gaussian example by using a measure based on two moments, using a finite number of moments will often be insufficient to capture all changes in a posterior distribution, which demonstrates the importance of having a strong measure of sensitivity.
Comparison with alternative sensitivity measures.
Having illustrated our method and discussed the importance of the strength of a sensitivity measure, we move on to computational considerations. In the following example, we further extend our analysis to multivariate Gaussians with
d
Оҳ
>
2
d_{\Theta}>2
and study the impact of the dimension of the posterior
d
Оҳ
d_{\Theta}
and the number of samples
m
m
on the estimation error and computational cost of different methods. We compare against three other measures of sensitivity: one based on the posterior mean, and two based on statistical divergences: the Wasserstein-2 distance and the KL divergence. Our results are given in
Figure
3
.
We first compare the FD and mean measures. These both have similar estimation errors of
рқ’Ә
вҖӢ
(
m
вҲ’
1
/
2
)
\mathcal{O}(m^{-1/2})
, and relatively small computational cost of
O
вҖӢ
(
m
вҖӢ
d
Оҳ
2
)
O(md_{\Theta}^{2})
and
рқ’Ә
вҖӢ
(
m
вҖӢ
d
Оҳ
)
\mathcal{O}(md_{\Theta})
respectively. Although the FD will have a larger cost for large
d
Оҳ
d_{\Theta}
, this has to be balanced with the fact that it controls changes in the entire posterior. In contrast, the two other divergence-based measures are both able to control changes in the entire posterior, but as discussed below, have estimation errors and computational costs which are orders of magnitude larger than that of the FD.
Firstly, the KL divergence is one of many statistical divergences used for sensitivity analysis, such as the Fisher-Rao
(Kurtek and Bharath,
2015
)
and Jensen-Shannon divergences
(Kallioinen
et al.
,
2024
)
, which require access to densities. This is typically not feasible for posterior distributions, where densities are only known up to normalisation constants. In those cases, it is first needed to obtain samples from both the reference and candidate posteriors, then to perform density estimation separately for the reference and candidate posteriors, and finally to plug-in these estimated densities to estimate the divergence through a Monte Carlo estimator. In
Figure
3
, this is achieved through kernel density estimation, which leads to a computational cost
O
вҖӢ
(
m
2
вҖӢ
d
Оҳ
)
O(m^{2}d_{\Theta})
and whose sample complexity is well known to suffer from the curse of dimensionality. This directly shows in the corresponding top row plot, where we do not observe convergence for
d
Оҳ
=
25
d_{\Theta}=25
and
d
Оҳ
=
100
d_{\Theta}=100
for
m
<
5000
m<5000
.
Secondly, we consider the Wasserstein-
2
2
distance, as studied by
Ghaderinezhad
et al.
(
2022
); Mingo
et al.
(
2026
)
. Here, we must sample from both the reference and candidate posterior, then estimate the divergence by computing the Wasserstein distance through optimal transport between the corresponding empirical measures. The computational complexity of this procedure is
рқ’Ә
вҖӢ
(
m
3
+

2
вҖӢ

Оҳ
)
\mathcal{O}(m^{3}+m^{2}d_{\Theta})
whenever

Оҳ
>

, which is significantly larger than all other alternative methods, and the sample complexity is
O
вҖӢ

Before concluding, we note that our study has considered the computational problem of estimating the measures of sensitivity for a fixed candidate, but in practice these will be used within numerical optimisation routines as in
Equation
4
. This will be an issue for all three of the mean, KL and Wasserstein-
2
2
sensitivity measures since they will each require re-sampling from each candidate distributions. This explains why existing work in global sensitivity analysis tends to almost exclusively consider discrete and finite neighbourhoods. In contrast, the FD only requires samples from
О 
~
ref
\tilde{\Pi}_{\text{ref}}
, and only uses the score of the candidates, which can typically be obtained relatively cheaply.
5.2
Measuring Sensitivity to Learning Rate Estimation Methods
We are now ready to move on to more realistic sensitivity problem.
One of the main difficulties faced in generalised Bayesian inference is the choice of how to balance the loss function and the prior, as these may be defined on different scales. This is typically achieved through a scalar hyperparameter called learning rate or temperature, which controls the relative importance of these quantities. Despite the importance of this problem, there is no consensus on how to select this hyperparameter, with numerous approaches having been proposed in the literature; see e.g
Wu and Martin (
2023
)
. It is also well established that generalised posteriors can be highly sensitive to the value of this hyperparameter, and that some of these methods can be somewhat unstable in low data regimes. Interestingly, our proposed FD sensitivity measure can be used to verify this sensitivity numerically.
To illustrate this, we consider inference for an Ising model, following the setup of Section 4.2 in
Matsubara
et al.
(
2024
)
. Despite only having one parameter, this is a challenging inference problem since the likelihood is only known in unnormalised form, which makes standard posteriors doubly intractable. The data in this experiment consist of binary configurations
x
вҲҲ
{
0
,
1
}
d
рқ’і
x\in\{0,1\}^{d_{\mathcal{X}}}
defined on a
6
Г—
6
6\times 6
grid graph, so that
d
рқ’і
=
36
d_{\mathcal{X}}=36
, and
p
Оё
вҖӢ
(
x
)
вҲқ
exp
вҒЎ
(
1
Оё
вҖӢ
вҲ‘
i
=
1
d
рқ’і
вҲ‘
j
вҲҲ
рқ’©
i
x
i
вҖӢ
x
j
)
p_{\theta}(x)\propto\exp(\frac{1}{\theta}\sum_{i=1}^{d_{\mathcal{X}}}\sum_{j\in\mathcal{N}_{i}}x_{i}x_{j})
where
Оё
вҲҲ
в„қ
+
\theta\in\mathbb{R}_{+}
and
рқ’©
i

denotes the neighbours of
x
i
x_{i}
on the graph.
We use a
ПҮ
2
\chi^{2}
prior with
3
3
degrees of freedom, and consider the dataset of size
n
=
1000
n=1000
provided in this paper. We consider three generalised posteriors obtained through the pseudo-likelihood
(Besag,
1974
)
, the discrete Fisher divergence (DFD;
Matsubara
et al.
(
2024
)
), and the discrete kernel Stein discrepancy (KSD;
Yang
et al.
(
2018
)
).
For each posterior, we used
m
=
5000
m=5000
samples from a reference posterior obtained via Hamiltonian Monte Carlo with
1000
1000
burn-in samples, which takes approximately 7 seconds. We also verify
1
and
3
in
Section
C.2.2
.
We compare three learning rate selection methods by
Syring and Martin (
2019
)
,
Lyddon
et al.
(
2019
)
and
Matsubara
et al.
(
2024
)
. These methods aim to estimate the learning rate so as to ensure the right frequentist coverage for credible regions of the generalised posterior. For each method, we consider the estimated learning rate value as our reference hyperparameter
О»
ref
\lambda_{\text{ref}}
, and measure sensitivity around it:
О“
=
{
О»
:
|
О»
вҲ’
О»
ref
|
вүӨ
Пө
}
\Gamma=\{\lambda:|\lambda-\lambda_{\text{ref}}|\leq\epsilon\}
, where
Пө
>
0
\epsilon>0
controls the size of the neighbourhood. This is particularly important because all three of these methods depend on algorithmic choices which can substantially affect the resulting estimate of the learning rate, such as a number of bootstrap replications or the number of steps of a numerical optimiser. The sensitivity measure should therefore tell us whether a given learning rate estimation method is stable for the problem at hand. The FD is particularly computationally convenient for this task; we see from
Equation
15
that for a loss of the form
L
вҖӢ
(
Оё
;

:

)

, the sensitivity measure simplifies to

вҖӢ
(
О“
)
=
Пө
2
вҖӢ
рқ”ј
Оё
вҲј

~

вҖӢ
[
вҖ–
вҲҮ

вҖӢ
(

;

:

)
вҖ–

]
S^{\mathrm{FD}}(\Gamma)=\epsilon^{2}\;\mathbb{E}_{\theta\sim\tilde{\Pi}_{\text{ref}}}\![\|\nabla_{\theta}l(\theta;x_{1:n})\|_{2}^{2}]
and therefore no optimisation is needed.
The results of our experiments are shown in

. The plots are informative for comparing calibration procedures for a fixed loss and dataset. We can see that the calibration procedure of

)
is the most sensitive since it leads to larger
ПҒ

Before concluding, we emphasise the computational efficiency with which such plots can be generated. Thanks to the simplified expression for FD in this example, we know that the sensitivity is quadratic in the size of the neighbourhood
Пө
\epsilon
, and that
ПҒ
^
m
FD
вҖӢ
(
О 
~
О»
L
)

can be estimated without any optimisation through the estimation of a single expectation from the MCMC samples from the reference. These figures therefore have negligible additional cost once sampling from a generalised posterior has been performed, and could therefore become a standard diagnostic in this field.
5.3
Autoregressive model of the temperature in KilpisjГӨrvi
For our next experiment, we return to the problem of prior sensitivity, but now consider the more challenging problem of Bayesian inference for time-series models, where sensitivity to hyperparameters is crucial
(Karlsson,
2013
; Giannone
et al.
,
2015
)
.
The aim of this experiment is twofold: to illustrate the method on a higher-dimensional posterior with real data, and to assess the performance of the FD sensitivity measure under different choices of optimisation routines.
We consider an autoregressive (AR) model for a univariate real-world time series of June temperatures recorded in KilpisjГӨrvi (Finland) starting in
1952
1952
, with
n
=
62
n=62
observations; see
Figure
6
. Such temperature records are commonly analysed to study long-term climate dynamics and potential warming trends in northern regions. Bayesian time-series models are particularly useful here because the amount of data is limited and there is uncertainty about the underlying temporal dependence structure, leading practitioners to use relatively vague priors. Therefore, prior sensitivity is important since downstream conclusions are based on posterior predictive distributions, and sensitivity to plausible prior perturbations may substantially affect predictive reliability.

One of the main advantages of our approach is that we obtain not only the sensitivity measure, but also the worst-case prior at which the supremum is obtained. As seen in

, the parameter
О»

S
^

вҖӢ

Interestingly, the impact of the worst-case prior can also be observed at the level of the posterior predictive distribution, as illustrated in
Figure
6
. Under the reference prior, the predictive trajectory appears relatively smooth and evolves gradually over time, whilst under the worst-case prior, the predictive trajectory becomes more oscillatory. This behaviour is consistent with the sensitivity analysis above: the posterior reacts the most for the perturbations of the short-lag autoregressive coefficients
ОІ
1
\beta_{1}
and
ОІ
2
\beta_{2}
. As a result, the model has a вҖңshorter memoryвҖқ, with predictions of the current observation being driven more strongly by the first and second lagged observations. The larger predictive uncertainty indicates that the worst case prior results in autoregressive dynamics that are less stable, providing yet another indication of the importance of sensitivity analysis.

Before concluding this example, we also comment on the computational cost of running our procedure, with results presented in
Figure
5(c)
.
We show three versions: a standard black-box dual annealing optimisation that directly solves the problem in
Equation
11
, a method which optimises by evaluating the quadratic-form objective in all
2
d
Оӣ
=
2
14
2^{d_{\Lambda}}=2^{14}
corner points of the box constraints, and finally a method which uses the decomposition of the sensitivity measure as a sum of quadratic forms to consider only
вҲ‘
j
=
1
d
Оҳ
2
d
Оӣ
j
=
28
\sum_{j=1}^{d_{\Theta}}2^{d_{\Lambda_{j}}}=28
corner configurations.
The black-box dual annealing approach runs for substantially longer than the alternatives, and is primarily used to benchmark the computational advantages of using the quadratic-form structure of our objective. Nevertheless, the computational cost of this approach is roughly similar to a single run of MCMC. In contrast, the most general quadratic form approach is around
500
500
times cheaper to run, whilst the sum of quadratic forms is around
1000
1000
times cheaper to run. This clearly demonstrates that the computational cost of performing sensitivity analysis is negligible relative to the cost of performing Bayesian inference with MCMC.

5.4
Simulation-based inference for calibrating radio propagation models
Our final example showcases our approach on a highly challenging Bayesian inference problem. We consider simulation-based inference (SBI) for the Turin radio propagation model

The Turin model is an early stochastic radio channel model which is commonly used to help engineers evaluate communication systems across different propagation environments without collecting costly real-world measurements. 6
CONCLUSION
This paper introduces a new computationally tractable method for global sensitivity analysis in Bayesian inference based on the Fisher divergence. By requiring only a single set of posterior samples and operating through score functions, our method directly addresses the key computational limitations that have hindered the practical use of Bayesian global sensitivity analysis. In many cases of practical relevance, we showed that the Fisher divergence is a convex quadratic form, which allows the associated optimisation problems to be solved efficiently. In addition, because the Fisher divergence considers discrepancies in the entire posterior, rather than merely summary statistics, it provides a strong measure of sensitivity which has high potential for challenging Bayesian inference problems.
We believe there are many possible and exciting extensions for the use of score-based divergences for Bayesian sensitivity analysis. The method could be readily applied across a wide range of Bayesian inference settings, including hierarchical models, as well as multi-task
(Guo
et al.
,
2011
)
, federated
(Yurochkin
et al.
,
2019
; Cao
et al.
,
2023
)
, and transfer learning
(Suder
et al.
,
2025
)
settings. These settings are particularly relevant because model parameters are organised across levels (e.g., global and task-specific), and the FD objective can therefore be simplified under a per-level decomposition. This could enable sensitivity analysis of different levels of the model, allowing the practitioner to identify, for instance, whether posterior instability is driven by shared hyperparameters or task-specific components, or whether heterogeneity across tasks amplifies sensitivity.
Beyond this, several methodological extensions could be pursued in future work. A first direction would be to address settings involving multimodal posteriors with well-separated modes, a scenario for which score-based divergences are known to perform poorly
(Wenliang and Kanagawa,
2020
; Zhang
et al.
,
2022
)
. One potential remedy is to construct the divergence using a tempered version of the reference posterior, thereby reducing the blindness to disjoint modes. A second direction would be to extend our measure beyond hyperparameter sensitivity. For instance,
Berger and Berliner (
1986
); Moreno and Pericchi (
1992
); Kurtek and Bharath (
2015
); Ho (
2023
)
study robustness under neighbourhoods defined by probability balls centred at a reference prior or likelihood; adapting our approach to these types of neighbourhoods could yield valuable insights. Finally, a third promising avenue would be to generalise the method beyond Euclidean parameter spaces to encompass posteriors defined over discrete domains, for which score functions are not naturally defined. In this case, the divergence proposed by
Laplante
et al.
(
2025
)
offers a principled alternative and also leads to a convex quadratic form. Such an extension would be particularly relevant for Bayesian model choice and variable selection problems involving discrete indices, as well as for hidden Markov models with discrete latent states.

The authors are thankful to Jeffrey Negrea, Chris Oates for helpful discussions, and to Ayush Bharti for support with the radio propagation example. AO was supported by a UCL EPSRC DTP Mathematical Science scholarship [EP/W524335/1], CD and FXB were supported by the EPSRC grant [EP/Y022300/1], and FXB was additionally supported by [EP/Y011805/1].

Supplementary Materials
The supplementary materials are structured as follows. In
Appendix
A
, we provide the proofs of all our theoretical results. In
Appendix
B
, we provide simplified expressions for the convex quadratic objective in various special cases. In
Appendix
C
, we provide additional numerical experiments to complement the results in the main text. Finally, in
Appendix
D
we briefly comment on the potential use of the FD in the context of local sensitivity analysis.
Appendix A

Appendix B
Special Cases: Measuring Sensitivity to either Prior or Loss
In this section, we discuss how the FD sensitivity measure simplifies if we only consider prior sensitivity or loss sensitivity, but not joint sensitivity. In each case, we discuss how the standing assumptions can be simplified, and we derive simplified quadratic form representations of the FD.
B.1

\displaystyle\left.\frac{d}{d\mu}\,\hat{\rho}_{\mathrm{FD}}(\tilde{\Pi}^{\lambda_{\mu}})\right|_{\mu=0}=-\frac{2}{m}\sum_{i=1}^{m}\big(s_{\tilde{\pi}_{\mathrm{ref}}}(\theta_{i})-s_{\tilde{\pi}^{\lambda_{\mu}}}(\theta_{i})\big)^{\top}\dot{s}_{\pi}(\theta_{i}|\lambda).

Instructions for reporting errors
We are continuing to improve HTML versions of papers, and your feedback helps enhance accessibility and mobile
      support. To report errors in the HTML that will help us improve conversion and rendering, choose any of the
      methods listed below:
Click the "Report Issue"
(
)
button, located in the page header.
Tip:
You can select the relevant text first, to include it in your report.
Our team has already identified
the following issues
. We appreciate your time reviewing and reporting rendering errors we
      may not have found yet. Your efforts will help us improve the HTML versions for all readers, because disability
      should not be a barrier to accessing research. Thank you for your continued support in championing open access for
      all.
Have a free development cycle? Help support accessibility at arXiv! Our collaborators at LaTeXML maintain a
list of packages that need conversion
, and welcome
developer contributions
